BRIEF RESEARCH REPORT article
 

Front. Psychol.

, 17 November 2022

 
 

 Volume 13 - 2022 | 
 

 

 
 

 




Role of fantasy in emotional clarity and emotional regulation in empathy: A preliminary study
 

 

 

 




Shoichi Shiota

1

&#x0002A;

&#x2020;



Michio Nomura
2

&#x2020;


 
 


1

Faculty of Human Relations, Tokai Gakuin University, Kakamigahara City, Gifu, Japan
 


2

Graduate School of Education, Kyoto University, Kyoto, Japan
 
Fantasy is the experience of identifying with characters in movies, novels, plays, and other fictional situations. In social contexts, individuals take on the perspective of others by sensing their emotions through empathy. During this process, perspective-taking and emotional sharing affect one&#x2019;s metacognition, which deals with the distinction between and the understanding of one&#x2019;s emotions (clarity) and their regulation (repair); previous studies have primarily focused on these processes. However, perspective-taking&#x2014;considering another individual&#x2019;s viewpoint&#x2014;requires one to imagine their outlook; it also induces emotional responses. This study examined the role of fantasy in clarity and repair in metacognition, for which data derived from 475 Japanese participants were analyzed. The results of the Interpersonal Reactivity Index showed that fantasy was positively associated with clarity and repair in the Trait Meta-Mood Scale; these relationships were moderated by perspective-taking and personal distress. Our results revealed that the emotions experienced within oneself might be understood as the distinction between &#x201C;imagining&#x201D; (through their imagination; e.g., internal or mental pictures) and &#x201C;imaging&#x201D; (from an image; e.g., external pictures). Individuals imagine their immersion into others using lower-level automatic body sensations (emotional contagion), and the accompanying negative emotions are regulated by metacognition.
 

 
 
 

Introduction
Metacognition is defined as the cognitive function of objectively monitoring one&#x2019;s inner experiences and emotional events, which vary momentarily (
Kabat-Zinn, 1994
). It consists of three aspects: clarity, which is related to the distinction between and understanding of one&#x2019;s emotions; repair, which is related to emotional regulation and improving negative emotions; and attention, which is related to defining one&#x2019;s feelings (
Gohm and Clore, 2002
). Metacognition refers to the activities involved in thinking and forming integrated ideas about oneself and others (
Bonfils et al., 2019
). Empathy is defined as the ability to experience and understand the momentary emotional state of another individual (
Salazar-L&#x000F3;pez et al., 2015
), and each component in empathy is closely contributed to metacognition. Fantasy is one of the empathy components that can be defined as the mental experiences associated with a tendency to identify with characters in movies, novels, plays, and other fictional situations (
Davis, 1980
, 
1983
). It is considered a central component of meaning (
Weibel et al., 2018
). According to 
Weibel et al. (2018)
, &#x201C;Fantasy offers a way of using cognitive functions for mental exploration and metacognition of counterfactual or expanded realms beyond the perceived &#x2018;real world.&#x201D; Therefore, it is suggested that fantasy in empathy is contributed to metacognition. However, previous studies have only examined the effects of perspective-taking and emotional sharing, which are components of empathy, on metacognition regarding clarity and repair (e.g., 
Decety and Meyer, 2008
). Accordingly, perspective-taking&#x2014;understanding the viewpoint of others&#x2014;requires one to imagine those perspectives. Hence, emotions are experienced within oneself by imagining those of others (
Eisenberg et al., 1988
; 
Barrett, 1992
; 
Stotland and Smith, 1994
; 
McInnis, 2014
). Based on these evidences, it is not only the relationships among fantasy, perspective-taking, and metacognition, that are suggested but also those of fantasy, emotional sharing, and metacognition. However, the details of these processes and the role of fantasy in them have not been studied extensively.

Salovey et al. (1995)
 developed an &#x201C;individual difference&#x201D; based measure of metacognition called the Trait Meta-Mood Scale (TMMS). The scale consists of three aspects: clarity, repair, and attention (
Gohm and Clore, 2002
). A previous study demonstrated a significant positive correlation between the inter-individual differences in scores for fantasy and clarity (
Gohm and Clore, 2002
). 
Thompson et al. (2017)
 reported that individuals with major depressive disorder and social anxiety disorder, including those with abnormal fantasies (
Rude et al., 2002
; 
Moscovitch et al., 2018
), scored lower than healthy subjects for both clarity and repair in the TMMS; both functions were also negatively correlated with clinical symptoms. Likewise, another study found a significant positive correlation between the inter-individual differences in the scores for fantasy and repair (
Shi and Wang, 2007
), while 
Eisenberg et al. (1988)
 reported a negative correlation between the inter-individual differences in scores for fantasy and sadness. These results suggest that fantasy directly contributes to clarity and repair in metacognition. However, the mechanisms of these processes are unknown.
Empathy is an important function of recognizing the emotional and mental states of others. The trait empathy of individuals is measured using the Interpersonal Reactivity Index (IRI; 
Davis, 1980
; 
Himichi et al., 2017
). The IRI is a self-report measure of the following aspects of empathy: fantasy and perspective-taking (cognitive empathy); and personal distress and empathic concern (affective empathy; 
Davis, 1983
). According to 
Davis (1980
, 
1983)
, perspective-taking items reflect the respondent&#x2019;s ability to adopt the perspectives of others. It leads one to anticipate the helping behaviors and reactions of others. Personal distress indicates that the respondent experiences discomfort and anxiety upon witnessing others having negative experiences. Empathic concern assesses a respondent&#x2019;s tendency to experience feelings of warmth, compassion, and concern for others undergoing negative experiences. By focusing on fantasy&#x2019;s relationship to perspective-taking and personal distress, 
Davis (1980)
 found that fantasy contributes to both perspective-taking and personal distress. 
McInnis (2014)
 revealed that high fantasy-orientated children had better scores for cognitive empathy and affective empathy than low fantasy-orientated children. In recent years, numerous studies have examined the relationships between perspective-taking and clarity and personal distress and repair (e.g., 
Decety and Lamm, 2011
). 
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az (2019)
 reported a positive correlation between perspective-taking in the IRI and clarity in the TMMS. 
Eckland et al. (2018)
 observed a significant positive correlation between cognitive empathy&#x2014;including fantasy and perspective-taking&#x2014;and clarity when considering the emotions generated by the observer, thereby suggesting that clarity in metacognition is affected by perspective-taking. A recent study that examined the relationship between personal distress and repair found a negative correlation between personal distress in the IRI and repair in the TMMS (
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019
). Repair in metacognition is affected by personal distress. In summary, these findings indicate that fantasy in empathy is positively associated with clarity and repair in metacognition; furthermore, this relationship is moderated by perspective-taking and personal distress.
Based on the literature, the relationships among fantasy in empathy, perspective taking in empathy, and clarity in metacognition are suggested. The relationships between fantasy in empathy, emotional sharing in empathy, and repair in metacognition are also suggested. Nevertheless, few studies have examined the details of these processes and the role of fantasy in them. The present study has academic value as it clarifies the role of fantasy in emotional clarity and emotional regulation while practicing empathy. We hypothesized the following:

H1
: Based on previous studies (
McInnis, 2014
; 
Thompson et al., 2017
; 
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019
), fantasy is positively associated with clarity in the Japanese version of the TMMS (TMMS-J), and this relationship is moderated by perspective-taking.

H2
: Based on previous studies (
Shi and Wang, 2007
; 
McInnis, 2014
; 
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019
), fantasy is positively associated with repair in the TMMS-J, and the relationship is moderated by personal distress.
 

Materials and methods
Participants
We used CrowdWorks



1

 to recruit 481 healthy Japanese participants who spoke Japanese as their native language and had no history of psychiatric disorders. They completed an online questionnaire distributed by Qualtrics, an online research company.



2

 First, the participants answered questions about demographics such as age, sex, and presence or absence of history of psychiatric disorders. Second, they answered the self-report questionnaires. Six participants were excluded from the analysis for providing incorrect responses. Thus, the final data of 475 healthy Japanese participants (242 women; aged 20&#x2013;75, mean age 39.4&#x2009;years, standard deviation [
SD
]&#x2009;=&#x2009;10.50) were available for analysis. We calculated the sample size by conducting a power analysis using G&#x0002A;Power (
Faul et al., 2007
) with the correlation coefficient (0.20), which was used to establish the criteria-related validity between the TMMS and other scales in a previous study (
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Berrocal et al., 2004
). Power analysis results indicated that at least 436 participants would be necessary for our analysis (effect size&#x2009;=&#x2009;
r
, &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.01, Power&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.95); thus, our sample size was sufficient. Before completing the questionnaire, all the participants provided their written informed consent.
Self-report questionnaires
Japanese version of the trait meta-mood scale
The original TMMS was developed by 
Salovey et al. (1995)
 to examine the ability of individuals to understand their moods and feelings, monitor the degree to which people moderate their moods, and determine the relationship between feelings and thoughts. The TMMS consists of three subscales: &#x201C;attention,&#x201D; &#x201C;clarity,&#x201D; and &#x201C;repair.&#x201D; In this context, &#x201C;attention&#x201D; is an individual&#x2019;s ability to define their feelings (e.g., &#x201C;I pay a lot of attention to how I feel&#x201D;); &#x201C;clarity&#x201D; is the ability to identify differences in emotions (e.g., &#x201C;I am usually very clear about my feelings&#x201D;); and &#x201C;repair&#x201D; is the capacity to improve negative emotions when required (e.g., &#x201C;I try to think good thoughts, no matter how bad I feel&#x201D;). The TMMS-J was translated and back-translated by native English speakers. We selected the following items from each subscale of the original TMMS based on pilot studies: attention: 7, 8, 13, 15, 35, 38, 41, and 46; clarity: 9, 12, 24, 28, 33, 37, 42, and 48; and repair: 2, 3, 10, 16, 17, 30, and 43. We conducted a confirmatory factor analysis of the TMMS-J scores. The result indicated a moderate to good fit to the model with the three-factor structure (&#x03C7;
2
 (227)&#x2009;=&#x2009;942.52, 
p
&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.001, GFI&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.835, CFI&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.814, RMSEA&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.082). The Cronbach&#x2019;s alpha was &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.81 for attention, &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.86 for clarity, and &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.71 for repair, thus indicating adequate internal consistency for each TMMS-J subscale in this study. The TMMS-J also had good internal consistency, equal to that of previous studies (attention; 
&#x03B1;
&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.76&#x2013;0.86; clarity 
&#x03B1;
&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.73&#x2013;0.87; repair &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.60&#x2013;0.82; 
Salovey et al., 1995
; 
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Berrocal et al., 2004
; 
Bugay et al., 2014
). The items were measured on a 5-point Likert scale ranging from 1 (&#x201C;Does not describe me well&#x201D;) to 5 (&#x201C;Describes me very well&#x201D;).
Japanese version of the interpersonal reactivity index
The Japanese version of IRI (IRI-J; 
Davis, 1980
; 
Himichi et al., 2017
) is a 28-item questionnaire widely used for the multidimensional assessment of trait empathy. It consists of four subscales: perspective taking, personal distress, empathic concern, and fantasy. Each of the four subscales comprises seven items that are assessed on a 5-point Likert scale ranging from 1 (&#x201C;Does not describe me well&#x201D;) to 5 (&#x201C;Describes me very well&#x201D;). Each subscale has a minimum total score of 7 or a maximum total score of 35. Perspective-taking evaluates an individual&#x2019;s tendency to adopt the psychological viewpoints of others (e.g., &#x201C;I sometimes try to understand my friends better by imagining how things look from their perspective&#x201D;). Personal distress is self-oriented and associated with aversive emotional responses in the observer (e.g., &#x201C;I tend to lose control during emergencies&#x201D;). Conversely, the empathic concern is other-oriented and relates to feelings of compassion and sympathy for the observed individual (e.g., &#x201C;When I see someone being taken advantage of, I feel protective toward them&#x201D;). Fantasy entails the participants&#x2019; abilities to transpose (immerse) themselves in fictional situations (e.g., &#x201C;When I am reading an interesting story or novel, I imagine how I would feel if the events in the story were happening to me&#x201D;). The mean and SDs for TMMS-J and IRI-J are shown in 
Table 1
. These values are similar to those of 
Himichi et al. (2017)
.

Mediation analysis
We next conducted a mediation analysis with fantasy as the independent variable, repair as the dependent variable, and personal distress as the mediating variance (
Figure 1C
). The results indicated that fantasy had a significant direct effect on repair (
&#x03B2;
&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.14; 
p
&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). The relationship between fantasy and repair was significantly mediated by personal distress (Sobel-test, 
Z
&#x2009;=&#x2009;3.52; 
p
&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). Moreover, fantasy had a direct effect on personal distress (
&#x03B2;
&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.11; 
p
&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05), and personal distress had a direct effect on repair (
&#x03B2;
&#x2009;=&#x2009;&#x02212;0.35; 
p
&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05).
 

Discussion
The results indicated that perspective-taking and personal distress contribute to clarity and repair, which is consistent with the findings of previous studies (e.g., 
Fern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019
). Notably, fantasy was positively associated with clarity when the perspective-taking score was high, thus confirming our first hypothesis (H1). Additionally, fantasy was positively associated with repair when the personal distress score was high, which confirmed our second hypothesis (H2). Further, the relationship between fantasy and repair was significantly mediated by personal distress. Notably, these results are not consistent with those of 
Decety and Lamm (2011)
. The pattern observed in our results suggests that comprehension of emotions is promoted by imagining the emotions of others.
According to 
Suzuki and Kino (2008)
, fantasy is a self-oriented function (e.g., the tendency to imagine being the other and thinking about &#x201C;how I feel&#x201D; as that person). Conversely, perspective-taking is an other-oriented function, and its purpose is to understand the observed individual (
Suzuki and Kino, 2008
). This cognitive process requires suppressing self-oriented thinking and considering the viewpoints of others (
Suzuki and Kino, 2008
). Previous studies (e.g., 
Decety and Meyer, 2008
) focused on the effect of perspective-taking on clarity in metacognition. In this study, fantasy was positively associated with clarity when the perspective-taking score was high. A few studies (
Taylor and Carlson, 1997
; 
Eckland et al., 2018
) provided similar findings. It is suggested that both fantasy and perspective-taking are essential components of distinguishing and understanding one&#x2019;s emotions.

Gallese and Goldman (1998)
 hypothesized that affective empathy entails &#x201C;simulation&#x201D; processes. Humans mirror others&#x2019; emotional responses to observed and experienced emotions in affective empathy. Our results suggested double dissociation between these &#x201C;simulation&#x201D; processes. In the first process, the results indicated that fantasy directly contributes to repair in metacognition; previous studies support these findings. For example, 
Eisenberg et al. (1988)
 reported a negative correlation between fantasy and sadness, which is a reaction to a sympathy experience. Similarly, 
Ferguson and Olson (2013)
 reported that playing fantasy video games reduces stress and enhances children&#x2019;s motivation, which is a function of resilience in the face of failure and the desire for social interaction. Previous and present findings established that fantasy reduces unpleasant emotions, enabling adaptive emotional regulation. In the second process, our results demonstrate that fantasy is related to emotional regulation, and this relationship is moderated by personal distress. Within the social cognitive domain, indicators of low self-other distinction are motor imitation and emotion contagion when we effectively take on the physical and emotional states of others (
Eddy, 2022
). Frequent emotion contagion may lead to emotional dysregulation; detachment from emotional experiences may help combat personal distress (
Eddy, 2022
). For example, during the last decade, there has been a growing body of research on the abnormality of fantasy and personal distress in empathy (e.g., 
Harari et al., 2010
; 
New et al., 2012
; 
De Meulemeester et al., 2021
). 
Harari et al. (2010)
 held that higher-order cognitive empathy processes, including fantasy and perspective-taking, which are impaired in borderline personality disorder, fail to modulate lower-level automatic emotional contagion, thus leading to elevated emotional empathy. 
De Meulemeester et al.&#x2019;s, 2021
 study showed that the ability to distinguish one&#x2019;s mental representations from those of others is a key feature of borderline personality disorder. Compared to healthy individuals, those with borderline personality disorder may immerse themselves in others more easily. This tendency may be due to the abnormality of affective empathy. Additionally, 
New et al. (2012)
 have shown that individuals with borderline personality disorder have abnormal experiences of personal distress. Personal distress has been described as affect sharing, similar to that seen in affective empathy but without a distinction between the self and other, thus resulting in self-oriented distress rather than an empathic reaction (
Preston and De Waal, 2002
; 
De Vignemont and Singer, 2006
; 
Decety and Jackson, 2006
). Based on previous studies (
Gallese and Goldman, 1998
; 
Harari et al., 2010
; 
New et al., 2012
; 
De Meulemeester et al., 2021
; 
Sowden et al., 2022
) and our results, an explanation for empathic personal distress may be that individuals imagine and immerse themselves in others&#x2019; experiences using lower-level automatic body sensations (emotional contagion), and the accompanying negative emotions are regulated by one&#x2019;s metacognition. On the other hand, frequency of personal distress could contribute to whether or not one engages in successful empathic interaction. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the double dissociations between &#x201C;simulation&#x201D; processes in affective empathy and the roles of fantasy and personal distress.
Although the importance of fantasy has been suggested in earlier research on empathy (
Stotland and Smith, 1994
), recent studies that have examined the relationship between empathy and metacognition have mainly focused on the relationships between perspective-taking and clarity, and personal distress and repair (e.g., 
Decety and Lamm, 2011
). However, the role of fantasy has not been considered thoroughly. Fantasy is like a canvas in one&#x2019;s mind, on which one can draw characters such as themselves or others. This canvas immerses people in a comprehensive image of an event. It is important to consider that elevated fantasies may not always demonstrate a beneficial effect (e.g., distracting from emotions, which may not always be helpful).
This study had several limitations. First, the fantasy scale in this study was one of the subscales of the IRI. We could not use other questionnaires that measured various aspects of fantasy. Furthermore, our study did not use an experimental design, and the result was not derived while participants were practicing empathy. Therefore, in the future, it is necessary to conduct behavioral and fMRI-based experiments incorporating positive and negative valences. Second, healthy Japanese individuals were recruited to complete the online survey. Building on previous studies that have reported on the abnormality of metacognition and fantasy (e.g., 
Semerari et al., 2003
; 
Rasmussen et al., 2017
), future research must examine the role of fantasy in both healthy and mentally ill populations alike. Third, our results indicate that fantasy was not associated with repair in a group with high personal distress scores. It implies the strength of the negative effect that personal distress has on emotional regulation. For example, feeling high levels of others&#x2019; pain diminishes one&#x2019;s ability to effectively carry out emotional self-regulation. Fourth, TMMS-J had only a moderately good model fit to the original three-factor structure. As previous review articles have suggested that there are cultural differences in the interpretation of and responses to emotions between people in Eastern and Western countries (
De Vaus et al., 2018
), it is of interest to conduct empirical research in different cultural contexts. Finally, metacognition is a comprehensive concept, with prior studies indicating the relationship between metacognition and autobiographical memory (e.g., 
Dimaggio et al., 2008
). Hence, an experimental paradigm that includes the effects of autobiographical memory must be developed in future studies. Despite these limitations, this study offered valuable insights into the mechanisms of empathy.
 

Data availability statement
The datasets presented in this study can be found in online repositories. The names of the repository/repositories and accession number(s) can be found in the article/
Supplementary material
.
 

Ethics statement
The studies involving human participants were reviewed and approved by the ethics committee of the Graduate School of Education, at Kyoto University. Written informed consent to participate in this study was provided by the participants&#x2019; legal guardian/next of kin.
 

Author contributions

class=\"author-wrapper notranslate\"\u003E\u003Cimg class=\"pr5\" src=\"https:\u002F\u002Floop.frontiersin.org\u002Fcdn\u002Fimages\u002Fprofile\u002Fdefault_32.jpg\" alt=\"Michio Nomura&#x;\" onerror=\"this.onerror=null;this.src='https:\u002F\u002Floop.frontiersin.org\u002Fcdn\u002Fimages\u002Fprofile\u002Fdefault_32.jpg';\"\u003EMichio Nomura\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E\u003Csup\u003E&#x2020;\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cul class=\"notes\"\u003E \u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Csup\u003E1\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003EFaculty of Human Relations, Tokai Gakuin University, Kakamigahara City, Gifu, Japan\u003C\u002Fli\u003E \u003Cli\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003EGraduate School of Education, Kyoto University, Kyoto, Japan\u003C\u002Fli\u003E \u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFantasy is the experience of identifying with characters in movies, novels, plays, and other fictional situations. In social contexts, individuals take on the perspective of others by sensing their emotions through empathy. During this process, perspective-taking and emotional sharing affect one&#x2019;s metacognition, which deals with the distinction between and the understanding of one&#x2019;s emotions (clarity) and their regulation (repair); previous studies have primarily focused on these processes. However, perspective-taking&#x2014;considering another individual&#x2019;s viewpoint&#x2014;requires one to imagine their outlook; it also induces emotional responses. This study examined the role of fantasy in clarity and repair in metacognition, for which data derived from 475 Japanese participants were analyzed. The results of the Interpersonal Reactivity Index showed that fantasy was positively associated with clarity and repair in the Trait Meta-Mood Scale; these relationships were moderated by perspective-taking and personal distress. Our results revealed that the emotions experienced within oneself might be understood as the distinction between &#x201C;imagining&#x201D; (through their imagination; e.g., internal or mental pictures) and &#x201C;imaging&#x201D; (from an image; e.g., external pictures). Individuals imagine their immersion into others using lower-level automatic body sensations (emotional contagion), and the accompanying negative emotions are regulated by metacognition.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"clear\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"JournalFullText\"\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h2\" name=\"h2\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EIntroduction\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003EMetacognition is defined as the cognitive function of objectively monitoring one&#x2019;s inner experiences and emotional events, which vary momentarily (\u003Ca href=\"#ref25\"\u003EKabat-Zinn, 1994\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). It consists of three aspects: clarity, which is related to the distinction between and understanding of one&#x2019;s emotions; repair, which is related to emotional regulation and improving negative emotions; and attention, which is related to defining one&#x2019;s feelings (\u003Ca href=\"#ref22\"\u003EGohm and Clore, 2002\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Metacognition refers to the activities involved in thinking and forming integrated ideas about oneself and others (\u003Ca href=\"#ref2\"\u003EBonfils et al., 2019\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Empathy is defined as the ability to experience and understand the momentary emotional state of another individual (\u003Ca href=\"#ref32\"\u003ESalazar-L&#x000F3;pez et al., 2015\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), and each component in empathy is closely contributed to metacognition. Fantasy is one of the empathy components that can be defined as the mental experiences associated with a tendency to identify with characters in movies, novels, plays, and other fictional situations (\u003Ca href=\"#ref4\"\u003EDavis, 1980\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#ref5\"\u003E1983\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). It is considered a central component of meaning (\u003Ca href=\"#ref43\"\u003EWeibel et al., 2018\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). According to \u003Ca href=\"#ref43\"\u003EWeibel et al. (2018)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, &#x201C;Fantasy offers a way of using cognitive functions for mental exploration and metacognition of counterfactual or expanded realms beyond the perceived &#x2018;real world.&#x201D; Therefore, it is suggested that fantasy in empathy is contributed to metacognition. However, previous studies have only examined the effects of perspective-taking and emotional sharing, which are components of empathy, on metacognition regarding clarity and repair (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref11\"\u003EDecety and Meyer, 2008\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Accordingly, perspective-taking&#x2014;understanding the viewpoint of others&#x2014;requires one to imagine those perspectives. Hence, emotions are experienced within oneself by imagining those of others (\u003Ca href=\"#ref15\"\u003EEisenberg et al., 1988\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref1\"\u003EBarrett, 1992\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref37\"\u003EStotland and Smith, 1994\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref26\"\u003EMcInnis, 2014\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Based on these evidences, it is not only the relationships among fantasy, perspective-taking, and metacognition, that are suggested but also those of fantasy, emotional sharing, and metacognition. However, the details of these processes and the role of fantasy in them have not been studied extensively.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003E\u003Ca href=\"#ref33\"\u003ESalovey et al. (1995)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E developed an &#x201C;individual difference&#x201D; based measure of metacognition called the Trait Meta-Mood Scale (TMMS). The scale consists of three aspects: clarity, repair, and attention (\u003Ca href=\"#ref22\"\u003EGohm and Clore, 2002\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). A previous study demonstrated a significant positive correlation between the inter-individual differences in scores for fantasy and clarity (\u003Ca href=\"#ref22\"\u003EGohm and Clore, 2002\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). \u003Ca href=\"#ref42\"\u003EThompson et al. (2017)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported that individuals with major depressive disorder and social anxiety disorder, including those with abnormal fantasies (\u003Ca href=\"#ref31\"\u003ERude et al., 2002\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref27\"\u003EMoscovitch et al., 2018\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), scored lower than healthy subjects for both clarity and repair in the TMMS; both functions were also negatively correlated with clinical symptoms. Likewise, another study found a significant positive correlation between the inter-individual differences in the scores for fantasy and repair (\u003Ca href=\"#ref35\"\u003EShi and Wang, 2007\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), while \u003Ca href=\"#ref15\"\u003EEisenberg et al. (1988)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported a negative correlation between the inter-individual differences in scores for fantasy and sadness. These results suggest that fantasy directly contributes to clarity and repair in metacognition. However, the mechanisms of these processes are unknown.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003EEmpathy is an important function of recognizing the emotional and mental states of others. The trait empathy of individuals is measured using the Interpersonal Reactivity Index (IRI; \u003Ca href=\"#ref4\"\u003EDavis, 1980\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref24\"\u003EHimichi et al., 2017\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). The IRI is a self-report measure of the following aspects of empathy: fantasy and perspective-taking (cognitive empathy); and personal distress and empathic concern (affective empathy; \u003Ca href=\"#ref5\"\u003EDavis, 1983\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). According to \u003Ca href=\"#ref4\"\u003EDavis (1980\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#ref5\"\u003E1983)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, perspective-taking items reflect the respondent&#x2019;s ability to adopt the perspectives of others. It leads one to anticipate the helping behaviors and reactions of others. Personal distress indicates that the respondent experiences discomfort and anxiety upon witnessing others having negative experiences. Empathic concern assesses a respondent&#x2019;s tendency to experience feelings of warmth, compassion, and concern for others undergoing negative experiences. By focusing on fantasy&#x2019;s relationship to perspective-taking and personal distress, \u003Ca href=\"#ref4\"\u003EDavis (1980)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E found that fantasy contributes to both perspective-taking and personal distress. \u003Ca href=\"#ref26\"\u003EMcInnis (2014)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E revealed that high fantasy-orientated children had better scores for cognitive empathy and affective empathy than low fantasy-orientated children. In recent years, numerous studies have examined the relationships between perspective-taking and clarity and personal distress and repair (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref10\"\u003EDecety and Lamm, 2011\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). \u003Ca href=\"#ref19\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az (2019)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported a positive correlation between perspective-taking in the IRI and clarity in the TMMS. \u003Ca href=\"#ref13\"\u003EEckland et al. (2018)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E observed a significant positive correlation between cognitive empathy&#x2014;including fantasy and perspective-taking&#x2014;and clarity when considering the emotions generated by the observer, thereby suggesting that clarity in metacognition is affected by perspective-taking. A recent study that examined the relationship between personal distress and repair found a negative correlation between personal distress in the IRI and repair in the TMMS (\u003Ca href=\"#ref19\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Repair in metacognition is affected by personal distress. In summary, these findings indicate that fantasy in empathy is positively associated with clarity and repair in metacognition; furthermore, this relationship is moderated by perspective-taking and personal distress.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EBased on the literature, the relationships among fantasy in empathy, perspective taking in empathy, and clarity in metacognition are suggested. The relationships between fantasy in empathy, emotional sharing in empathy, and repair in metacognition are also suggested. Nevertheless, few studies have examined the details of these processes and the role of fantasy in them. The present study has academic value as it clarifies the role of fantasy in emotional clarity and emotional regulation while practicing empathy. We hypothesized the following:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cblockquote\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003E\u003Ci\u003EH1\u003C\u002Fi\u003E: Based on previous studies (\u003Ca href=\"#ref26\"\u003EMcInnis, 2014\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref42\"\u003EThompson et al., 2017\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref19\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), fantasy is positively associated with clarity in the Japanese version of the TMMS (TMMS-J), and this relationship is moderated by perspective-taking.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fblockquote\u003E\n\u003Cblockquote\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003E\u003Ci\u003EH2\u003C\u002Fi\u003E: Based on previous studies (\u003Ca href=\"#ref35\"\u003EShi and Wang, 2007\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref26\"\u003EMcInnis, 2014\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref19\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), fantasy is positively associated with repair in the TMMS-J, and the relationship is moderated by personal distress.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fblockquote\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h3\" name=\"h3\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EMaterials and methods\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003EParticipants\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EWe used CrowdWorks\u003Csup id=\"footnotesuper1\"\u003E\u003Ca id=\"xfn0005\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Ca class=\"footnoteanchor\" href=\"#fn0005\"\u003E1\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E to recruit 481 healthy Japanese participants who spoke Japanese as their native language and had no history of psychiatric disorders. They completed an online questionnaire distributed by Qualtrics, an online research company.\u003Csup id=\"footnotesuper2\"\u003E\u003Ca id=\"xfn0006\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Ca class=\"footnoteanchor\" href=\"#fn0006\"\u003E2\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E First, the participants answered questions about demographics such as age, sex, and presence or absence of history of psychiatric disorders. Second, they answered the self-report questionnaires. Six participants were excluded from the analysis for providing incorrect responses. Thus, the final data of 475 healthy Japanese participants (242 women; aged 20&#x2013;75, mean age 39.4&#x2009;years, standard deviation [\u003Ci\u003ESD\u003C\u002Fi\u003E]&#x2009;=&#x2009;10.50) were available for analysis. We calculated the sample size by conducting a power analysis using G&#x0002A;Power (\u003Ca href=\"#ref16\"\u003EFaul et al., 2007\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) with the correlation coefficient (0.20), which was used to establish the criteria-related validity between the TMMS and other scales in a previous study (\u003Ca href=\"#ref20\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Berrocal et al., 2004\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Power analysis results indicated that at least 436 participants would be necessary for our analysis (effect size&#x2009;=&#x2009;\u003Ci\u003Er\u003C\u002Fi\u003E, &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.01, Power&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.95); thus, our sample size was sufficient. Before completing the questionnaire, all the participants provided their written informed consent.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003ESelf-report questionnaires\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Ch4\u003EJapanese version of the trait meta-mood scale\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThe original TMMS was developed by \u003Ca href=\"#ref33\"\u003ESalovey et al. (1995)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to examine the ability of individuals to understand their moods and feelings, monitor the degree to which people moderate their moods, and determine the relationship between feelings and thoughts. The TMMS consists of three subscales: &#x201C;attention,&#x201D; &#x201C;clarity,&#x201D; and &#x201C;repair.&#x201D; In this context, &#x201C;attention&#x201D; is an individual&#x2019;s ability to define their feelings (e.g., &#x201C;I pay a lot of attention to how I feel&#x201D;); &#x201C;clarity&#x201D; is the ability to identify differences in emotions (e.g., &#x201C;I am usually very clear about my feelings&#x201D;); and &#x201C;repair&#x201D; is the capacity to improve negative emotions when required (e.g., &#x201C;I try to think good thoughts, no matter how bad I feel&#x201D;). The TMMS-J was translated and back-translated by native English speakers. We selected the following items from each subscale of the original TMMS based on pilot studies: attention: 7, 8, 13, 15, 35, 38, 41, and 46; clarity: 9, 12, 24, 28, 33, 37, 42, and 48; and repair: 2, 3, 10, 16, 17, 30, and 43. We conducted a confirmatory factor analysis of the TMMS-J scores. The result indicated a moderate to good fit to the model with the three-factor structure (&#x03C7;\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E (227)&#x2009;=&#x2009;942.52, \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.001, GFI&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.835, CFI&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.814, RMSEA&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.082). The Cronbach&#x2019;s alpha was &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.81 for attention, &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.86 for clarity, and &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.71 for repair, thus indicating adequate internal consistency for each TMMS-J subscale in this study. The TMMS-J also had good internal consistency, equal to that of previous studies (attention; \u003Ci\u003E&#x03B1;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.76&#x2013;0.86; clarity \u003Ci\u003E&#x03B1;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.73&#x2013;0.87; repair &#x03B1;&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.60&#x2013;0.82; \u003Ca href=\"#ref33\"\u003ESalovey et al., 1995\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref20\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Berrocal et al., 2004\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref3\"\u003EBugay et al., 2014\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). The items were measured on a 5-point Likert scale ranging from 1 (&#x201C;Does not describe me well&#x201D;) to 5 (&#x201C;Describes me very well&#x201D;).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch4\u003EJapanese version of the interpersonal reactivity index\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThe Japanese version of IRI (IRI-J; \u003Ca href=\"#ref4\"\u003EDavis, 1980\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref24\"\u003EHimichi et al., 2017\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) is a 28-item questionnaire widely used for the multidimensional assessment of trait empathy. It consists of four subscales: perspective taking, personal distress, empathic concern, and fantasy. Each of the four subscales comprises seven items that are assessed on a 5-point Likert scale ranging from 1 (&#x201C;Does not describe me well&#x201D;) to 5 (&#x201C;Describes me very well&#x201D;). Each subscale has a minimum total score of 7 or a maximum total score of 35. Perspective-taking evaluates an individual&#x2019;s tendency to adopt the psychological viewpoints of others (e.g., &#x201C;I sometimes try to understand my friends better by imagining how things look from their perspective&#x201D;). Personal distress is self-oriented and associated with aversive emotional responses in the observer (e.g., &#x201C;I tend to lose control during emergencies&#x201D;). Conversely, the empathic concern is other-oriented and relates to feelings of compassion and sympathy for the observed individual (e.g., &#x201C;When I see someone being taken advantage of, I feel protective toward them&#x201D;). Fantasy entails the participants&#x2019; abilities to transpose (immerse) themselves in fictional situations (e.g., &#x201C;When I am reading an interesting story or novel, I imagine how I would feel if the events in the story were happening to me&#x201D;). The mean and SDs for TMMS-J and IRI-J are shown in \u003Ca href=\"#tab1\"\u003ETable 1\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. These values are similar to those of \u003Ca href=\"#ref24\"\u003EHimichi et al. (2017)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\"\u003ETABLE 1\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv loading=\"lazy\"\u003E\n  \u003C\u002Fpicture\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ETable 1\u003C\u002Fb\u003E. Means and \u003Ci\u003ESD\u003C\u002Fi\u003Es for each scale (\u003Ci\u003En\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;475).\n\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"clear\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h4\" name=\"h4\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EResults\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003EHierarchical multiple regression analyses\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EHierarchical multiple regression analyses were performed to examine the main effect of each subcomponent of empathy and metacognition, along with their interactive effects. Gender and age were entered into the model as the control variables in Step 1. The main effect and interaction of each subscale in the IRI-J were entered in Step 2. The results are summarized in \u003Ca href=\"#tab2\"\u003ETable 2\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. After controlling for the demographic variables, the main effect and interaction between each subscale in the IRI-J were explored as potential predictors of the variance in clarity in the TMMS-J (&#x0394;\u003Ci\u003ER\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.34; \u003Ci\u003EF\u003C\u002Fi\u003E [10, 462]&#x2009;=&#x2009;22.64; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). The main effect of personal distress in the IRI-J (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;&#x02212;0.58; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[&#x02212;0.729, &#x02212;0.554]) and interaction between perspective-taking and fantasy in the IRI-J (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.11; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[0.006, 0.055]) significantly predicted clarity in the TMMS-J. This interaction was examined using a simple slope analysis.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\"\u003ETABLE Hierarchical multiple linear regression predicting clarity and repair in the TMMS-J from each subscale in the IRI-J (\u003Ci\u003EN\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;475).\n\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"clear\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003ESubsequently, based on a previous study (\u003Ca href=\"#ref40\"\u003ETakano et al., 2018\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), we split the moderator using &#x201C;median&#x2009;&#x000B1;&#x2009;1SD&#x201D; in the simple slope analysis. The results showed that fantasy was positively associated with clarity in the high-scores group for perspective-taking scores in the IRI-J (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.12, \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). However, this relationship was not significant in the corresponding low-scores group (perspective-taking in the IRI-J; \u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.06, \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.32; \u003Ca href=\"#fig1\"\u003EFigure 1A\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). After controlling for the demographic variables, the main effect and interaction of each subscale in the IRI-J were explored as significant predictors of the variance in repair (&#x0394;\u003Ci\u003ER\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.25; \u003Ci\u003EF\u003C\u002Fi\u003E [10, 462]&#x2009;=&#x2009;22.64; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). The main effect of perspective-taking (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.21; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[0.127, 0.321]), personal distress (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;&#x02212;0.35; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[&#x02212;0.369, &#x02212;0.226]), empathic concern (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.12; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[0.026, 0.202]), and the interaction between personal distress and fantasy (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.11; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[0.003, 0.032]) significantly predicted repair.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cdiv loading=\"lazy\"\u003E\n  \u003C\u002Fpicture\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EFigure 1\u003C\u002Fb\u003E. The relationship between fantasy, perspective-taking, personal distress, metacognitive clarity, and metacognitive repair. \u003Cb\u003E(A)\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Moderating effect of perspective-taking on the relationship between fantasy in the IRI-J and clarity in the TMMS-J. \u003Cb\u003E(B)\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Moderating effect of personal distress on the relationship between fantasy in the IRI-J and repair in the TMMS-J. \u003Cb\u003E(C)\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Mediation model of fantasy in the IRI-J, personal distress in the IRI-J, and repair in the TMMS-J.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"clear\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThis significant interaction was further examined \u003Ci\u003Evia\u003C\u002Fi\u003E a simple slope analysis. The results showed that fantasy was positively associated with repair in the low-scores group for personal distress (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.17; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). However, this relationship was not significant in the corresponding high-scores group (personal distress in the IRI-J; \u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;&#x02212;0.01, \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.91; \u003Ca href=\"#fig1\"\u003EFigure 1B\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). After controlling for the demographic variables, the main effect and interaction of each subscale in the IRI-J were explored as significant predictors of the variance in attention in the TMMS-J (&#x0394;\u003Ci\u003ER\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.23; \u003Ci\u003EF\u003C\u002Fi\u003E[10, 462]&#x2009;=&#x2009;22.64; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). The main effect of perspective-taking (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.11; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[0.017, 0.203]), empathic concern (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;&#x02212;0.31; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[&#x02212;0.195, 0.364]), and fantasy (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.20; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05; 95% CI&#x2009;=&#x2009;[0.104, 0.267]) significantly predicted attention.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Ch3\u003EMediation analysis\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EWe next conducted a mediation analysis with fantasy as the independent variable, repair as the dependent variable, and personal distress as the mediating variance (\u003Ca href=\"#fig1\"\u003EFigure 1C\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). The results indicated that fantasy had a significant direct effect on repair (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.14; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). The relationship between fantasy and repair was significantly mediated by personal distress (Sobel-test, \u003Ci\u003EZ\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;3.52; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05). Moreover, fantasy had a direct effect on personal distress (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;0.11; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05), and personal distress had a direct effect on repair (\u003Ci\u003E&#x03B2;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;=&#x2009;&#x02212;0.35; \u003Ci\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2009;&#x0003C;&#x2009;0.05).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h5\" name=\"h5\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EDiscussion\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003EThe results indicated that perspective-taking and personal distress contribute to clarity and repair, which is consistent with the findings of previous studies (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref19\"\u003EFern&#x000E1;ndez-Abascal and Mart&#x000ED;n-D&#x000ED;az, 2019\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Notably, fantasy was positively associated with clarity when the perspective-taking score was high, thus confirming our first hypothesis (H1). Additionally, fantasy was positively associated with repair when the personal distress score was high, which confirmed our second hypothesis (H2). Further, the relationship between fantasy and repair was significantly mediated by personal distress. Notably, these results are not consistent with those of \u003Ca href=\"#ref10\"\u003EDecety and Lamm (2011)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. The pattern observed in our results suggests that comprehension of emotions is promoted by imagining the emotions of others.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003EAccording to \u003Ca href=\"#ref39\"\u003ESuzuki and Kino (2008)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, fantasy is a self-oriented function (e.g., the tendency to imagine being the other and thinking about &#x201C;how I feel&#x201D; as that person). Conversely, perspective-taking is an other-oriented function, and its purpose is to understand the observed individual (\u003Ca href=\"#ref39\"\u003ESuzuki and Kino, 2008\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). This cognitive process requires suppressing self-oriented thinking and considering the viewpoints of others (\u003Ca href=\"#ref39\"\u003ESuzuki and Kino, 2008\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Previous studies (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref11\"\u003EDecety and Meyer, 2008\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) focused on the effect of perspective-taking on clarity in metacognition. In this study, fantasy was positively associated with clarity when the perspective-taking score was high. A few studies (\u003Ca href=\"#ref41\"\u003ETaylor and Carlson, 1997\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref13\"\u003EEckland et al., 2018\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) provided similar findings. It is suggested that both fantasy and perspective-taking are essential components of distinguishing and understanding one&#x2019;s emotions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003E\u003Ca href=\"#ref21\"\u003EGallese and Goldman (1998)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E hypothesized that affective empathy entails &#x201C;simulation&#x201D; processes. Humans mirror others&#x2019; emotional responses to observed and experienced emotions in affective empathy. Our results suggested double dissociation between these &#x201C;simulation&#x201D; processes. In the first process, the results indicated that fantasy directly contributes to repair in metacognition; previous studies support these findings. For example, \u003Ca href=\"#ref15\"\u003EEisenberg et al. (1988)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported a negative correlation between fantasy and sadness, which is a reaction to a sympathy experience. Similarly, \u003Ca href=\"#ref18\"\u003EFerguson and Olson (2013)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E reported that playing fantasy video games reduces stress and enhances children&#x2019;s motivation, which is a function of resilience in the face of failure and the desire for social interaction. Previous and present findings established that fantasy reduces unpleasant emotions, enabling adaptive emotional regulation. In the second process, our results demonstrate that fantasy is related to emotional regulation, and this relationship is moderated by personal distress. Within the social cognitive domain, indicators of low self-other distinction are motor imitation and emotion contagion when we effectively take on the physical and emotional states of others (\u003Ca href=\"#ref14\"\u003EEddy, 2022\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Frequent emotion contagion may lead to emotional dysregulation; detachment from emotional experiences may help combat personal distress (\u003Ca href=\"#ref14\"\u003EEddy, 2022\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). For example, during the last decade, there has been a growing body of research on the abnormality of fantasy and personal distress in empathy (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref23\"\u003EHarari et al., 2010\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref28\"\u003ENew et al., 2012\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref6\"\u003EDe Meulemeester et al., 2021\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). \u003Ca href=\"#ref23\"\u003EHarari et al. (2010)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E held that higher-order cognitive empathy processes, including fantasy and perspective-taking, which are impaired in borderline personality disorder, fail to modulate lower-level automatic emotional contagion, thus leading to elevated emotional empathy. \u003Ca href=\"#ref6\"\u003EDe Meulemeester et al.&#x2019;s, 2021\u003C\u002Fa\u003E study showed that the ability to distinguish one&#x2019;s mental representations from those of others is a key feature of borderline personality disorder. Compared to healthy individuals, those with borderline personality disorder may immerse themselves in others more easily. This tendency may be due to the abnormality of affective empathy. Additionally, \u003Ca href=\"#ref28\"\u003ENew et al. (2012)\u003C\u002Fa\u003E have shown that individuals with borderline personality disorder have abnormal experiences of personal distress. Personal distress has been described as affect sharing, similar to that seen in affective empathy but without a distinction between the self and other, thus resulting in self-oriented distress rather than an empathic reaction (\u003Ca href=\"#ref29\"\u003EPreston and De Waal, 2002\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref8\"\u003EDe Vignemont and Singer, 2006\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref9\"\u003EDecety and Jackson, 2006\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Based on previous studies (\u003Ca href=\"#ref21\"\u003EGallese and Goldman, 1998\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref23\"\u003EHarari et al., 2010\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref28\"\u003ENew et al., 2012\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref6\"\u003EDe Meulemeester et al., 2021\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref36\"\u003ESowden et al., 2022\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) and our results, an explanation for empathic personal distress may be that individuals imagine and immerse themselves in others&#x2019; experiences using lower-level automatic body sensations (emotional contagion), and the accompanying negative emotions are regulated by one&#x2019;s metacognition. On the other hand, frequency of personal distress could contribute to whether or not one engages in successful empathic interaction. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the double dissociations between &#x201C;simulation&#x201D; processes in affective empathy and the roles of fantasy and personal distress.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\"\u003EAlthough the importance of fantasy has been suggested in earlier research on empathy (\u003Ca href=\"#ref37\"\u003EStotland and Smith, 1994\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), recent studies that have examined the relationship between empathy and metacognition have mainly focused on the relationships between perspective-taking and clarity, and personal distress and repair (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref10\"\u003EDecety and Lamm, 2011\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). However, the role of fantasy has not been considered thoroughly. Fantasy is like a canvas in one&#x2019;s mind, on which one can draw characters such as themselves or others. This canvas immerses people in a comprehensive image of an event. It is important to consider that elevated fantasies may not always demonstrate a beneficial effect (e.g., distracting from emotions, which may not always be helpful).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThis study had several limitations. First, the fantasy scale in this study was one of the subscales of the IRI. We could not use other questionnaires that measured various aspects of fantasy. Furthermore, our study did not use an experimental design, and the result was not derived while participants were practicing empathy. Therefore, in the future, it is necessary to conduct behavioral and fMRI-based experiments incorporating positive and negative valences. Second, healthy Japanese individuals were recruited to complete the online survey. Building on previous studies that have reported on the abnormality of metacognition and fantasy (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref34\"\u003ESemerari et al., 2003\u003C\u002Fa\u003E; \u003Ca href=\"#ref30\"\u003ERasmussen et al., 2017\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), future research must examine the role of fantasy in both healthy and mentally ill populations alike. Third, our results indicate that fantasy was not associated with repair in a group with high personal distress scores. It implies the strength of the negative effect that personal distress has on emotional regulation. For example, feeling high levels of others&#x2019; pain diminishes one&#x2019;s ability to effectively carry out emotional self-regulation. Fourth, TMMS-J had only a moderately good model fit to the original three-factor structure. As previous review articles have suggested that there are cultural differences in the interpretation of and responses to emotions between people in Eastern and Western countries (\u003Ca href=\"#ref7\"\u003EDe Vaus et al., 2018\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), it is of interest to conduct empirical research in different cultural contexts. Finally, metacognition is a comprehensive concept, with prior studies indicating the relationship between metacognition and autobiographical memory (e.g., \u003Ca href=\"#ref12\"\u003EDimaggio et al., 2008\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). Hence, an experimental paradigm that includes the effects of autobiographical memory must be developed in future studies. Despite these limitations, this study offered valuable insights into the mechanisms of empathy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h6\" name=\"h6\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EData availability statement\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThe datasets presented in this study can be found in online repositories. The names of the repository\u002Frepositories and accession number(s) can be found in the article\u002F\u003Ca href=\"#h13\"\u003ESupplementary material\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h7\" name=\"h7\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EEthics statement\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThe studies involving human participants were reviewed and approved by the ethics committee of the Graduate School of Education, at Kyoto University. Written informed consent to participate in this study was provided by the participants&#x2019; legal guardian\u002Fnext of kin.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h8\" name=\"h8\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EAuthor contributions\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003ESS, MN: conceived and designed the study and contributed to the writing of the manuscript. SS: performed the recruitment and survey. All authors contributed to the article and approved the submitted version.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h9\" name=\"h9\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EFunding\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EThis study was supported by the Grant-in-Aid for the Japan Society for the Promotion of Science (JSPS) fellows (18&#x2009;J01157).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ca id=\"h10\" name=\"h10\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\n\u003Ch2\u003EAcknowledgments\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\"\u003EWe would like to express our gratitude to everyone at the Graduate School of Education, Kyoto University, for teaching us important lessons when we needed them the most. in Psychology","PDF",5,36,"psychology",4,2,"description","Frontiers","Help center","Link","Grey","Medium","ssph-journal.org","fship","Front. Psychol.","1664-1078",void 0,"Role of fantasy in emotional clarity and emotional regulation in empathy: A preliminary study",18,13,1920,"por-journal.com",7,"escubed.org",1918,"fipp","https:\u002F\u002Fd2csxpduxe849s.cloudfront.net\u002Fmedia\u002FE32629C6-9347-4F84-81FEAEF7BFA342B3\u002F0F39389F-DE2D-4BF1-AFE71BA9B6F25024\u002Fwebimage-58DE3D89-E16A-4C93-B1B7ACD8DCE23BE3.jpg","image","2022-06-27T10:00:51Z","fpsyg",69,"journal_field","10.3389\u002Ffpsyg.2022.912165","\u003Cp\u003EFantasy is the experience of identifying with characters in movies, novels, plays, and other fictional situations. In social contexts, individuals take on the perspective of others by sensing their emotions through empathy. During this process, perspective-taking and emotional sharing affect one’s metacognition, which deals with the distinction between and the understanding of one’s emotions (clarity) and their regulation (repair); previous studies have primarily focused on these processes. However, perspective-taking—considering another individual’s viewpoint—requires one to imagine their outlook; it also induces emotional responses. This study examined the role of fantasy in clarity and repair in metacognition, for which data derived from 475 Japanese participants were analyzed. The results of the Interpersonal Reactivity Index showed that fantasy was positively associated with clarity and repair in the Trait Meta-Mood Scale; these relationships were moderated by perspective-taking and personal distress. Our results revealed that the emotions experienced within oneself might be understood as the distinction between “imagining” (through their imagination; e.g., internal or mental pictures) and “imaging” (from an image; e.g., external pictures). Individuals imagine their immersion into others using lower-level automatic body sensations (emotional contagion), and the accompanying negative emotions are regulated by metacognition.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E",595103,"Shoichi","Japan","Michio",612362,601033,"Paula",356041,"Lloyd",{},45,"Personality and Social Psychology","personality-and-social-psychology","EPUB","fpsyg-13-912165.pdf","Frontiers | Role of fantasy in emotional clarity and emotional regulation in empathy: A preliminary study","https:\u002F\u002Fwww.frontiersin.org\u002Fjournals\u002Fpsychology\u002Farticles\u002F10.3389\u002Ffpsyg.2022.912165\u002Ffull","Fantasy is the experience of identifying with characters in movies, novels, plays, and other fictional situations.
